Specification 01 — Consensus and Double-Spend Architecture
1. Purpose

This specification defines how the system prevents double spending, orders conflicting history, and produces usable finality without handing control to a visible validator class, prior wealth holders, or custodial intermediaries.

This spec is intentionally narrow.
It only covers:

base-layer consensus,

block / DAG ordering,

conflict resolution,

finality policy,

miner incentives,

and the interaction between consensus and private transactions.

It does not yet finalize:

the proof system,

the stable unit,

the fast payments layer,

or selective disclosure grammar.

2. Design thesis

The base layer must answer one question better than anything else:

how does the network decide which spend is real when two incompatible spends exist, without requiring trust in validators, custodians, stake cartels, or a visible committee?

The architecture therefore rejects stake-first consensus as the base validity mechanism. Current systems already show the trade-off clearly: IOTA Rebased chose a dPoS / staking / validator-set model, and Penumbra is a private PoS system with a bounded active validator set and validator-visible governance. Those can be useful for other goals, but they are poor templates for a sovereign base layer whose main job is to resist capture and not convert wealth into protocol authority. Monero, by contrast, shows why privacy-first settlement matters, even while its own wallet modes also illustrate that ledger privacy alone is insufficient.

3. Scope

This specification covers:

the consensus resource,

the base data structure for history,

canonical ordering of blocks,

canonical ordering of private spends,

block acceptance,

reorg handling,

confirmation policy,

miner behavior and incentives,

anti-spam at the consensus boundary,

and consensus-layer failure modes.

4. Non-goals

This specification does not attempt to optimize for:

raw TPS marketing;

single-confirmation retail UX;

zero-latency settlement;

“greenest possible” narrative;

or compatibility with validator-driven governance models.

If any of those goals conflict with sovereignty, anti-capture, or anti-double-spend integrity, they lose.

5. Hard requirements

The consensus architecture must satisfy all of the following.

5.1 No authority by prior wealth

Consensus power must not be derived directly from ownership of the native asset.

5.2 No visible mandatory validator class

The system must not require a bounded, named, or semi-static validator committee to determine transaction validity.

5.3 Externalized security cost

Rewriting history must require an external cost, not just internal governance or social preference.

5.4 Probabilistic but operational finality

The system may use probabilistic finality, but it must expose a clear operational rule for wallets, merchants, and infrastructure.

5.5 Private-spend conflict resolution

The system must resolve double spends even when transaction internals are hidden.

5.6 Compatibility with private transactions

Consensus must not require transparent balances or transparent ownership graphs.

5.7 Bounded complexity

The ordering and conflict-resolution rules must remain auditable by independent teams.

6. Adversary model

The consensus layer assumes adversaries that may be:

capital-rich,

infrastructure-rich,

politically connected,

miner-colluding,

able to run surveillance peers,

and willing to accept losses to damage credibility.

It assumes the following attack classes are relevant:

double spend,

selfish mining,

withholding,

censorship by mining cartel,

reorg abuse,

eclipse-assisted timing,

spam to increase confirmation uncertainty,

and regulatory pressure on visible consensus actors.

It does not assume perfect network synchrony.

7. Candidate families evaluated

The relevant candidate families were:

A. PoW linear chain

Simple, auditable, battle-tested geometry.
Weakness: limited parallelism and more obvious throughput ceiling.

B. PoW blockDAG

Parallel block production with deterministic conflict ordering.
Weakness: higher complexity and more room for subtle implementation failure.

C. PoS / dPoS / committee BFT

Rejected for the base layer.

D. Hybrid PoW/PoS

Rejected provisionally because it tends to stack capture surfaces rather than remove them.

8. Rejection of stake-based base validity

Stake-based systems fail this design for structural reasons:

they convert prior wealth into consensus power,

they produce visible or semi-visible governance actors,

they invite cartelization through custodians and staking intermediaries,

and they are easier to pressure through law, licensing, and operations than diffuse PoW participants.

This is not a claim that they are useless.
It is a claim that they are misaligned with the base-layer mission.

9. Decision framework: linear chain vs blockDAG

The real decision is not “PoW or PoS” anymore.

It is:

should the sovereign PoW base use a linear history geometry, or a parallelized blockDAG with deterministic canonical ordering?

That is the live choice.

10. Baseline recommendation
10.1 Provisional architectural choice

Preferred target: PoW blockDAG with deterministic canonical block ordering and private-spend conflict resolution via public nullifiers.

10.2 Fallback if complexity fails

Fallback reference: PoW linear chain with the same private-spend model.

This means the project should aim for a blockDAG base, but only keep it if it survives formal and adversarial review. If the ordering logic, privacy interaction, or light-client model becomes too brittle, the design must fall back to a linear chain rather than ship a clever failure.

11. Why blockDAG is preferred

The reason is not ideology. It is pressure from the rest of the architecture.

A privacy-heavy system already pays costs in:

proof size or verification burden,

client complexity,

light-client design,

and merchant confirmation uncertainty.

A linear chain adds another bottleneck: serialized block production.

A blockDAG gives the base layer room to breathe by allowing more parallel block production while still anchoring security to PoW. That gives three strategic advantages:

better throughput headroom for a privacy-heavy base layer,

lower waste from simultaneous honest block production,

and better fit with a world where global propagation is imperfect and miners do not see the same frontier at the same instant.

That said, none of those are free. The price is higher ordering complexity.

12. Why blockDAG is dangerous

This is where most proposals start lying.

A blockDAG is not automatically better. It is only better if all of these survive scrutiny:

canonical order remains deterministic,

hidden-value transactions do not make conflict resolution ambiguous,

light clients can still verify relevant state safely,

anti-spam rules remain simple enough,

and implementation complexity does not centralize understanding in a priesthood.

If those conditions fail, a blockDAG stops being an advantage and becomes a fragility multiplier.

So the correct posture is:

prefer it, distrust it, and force it to earn survival.

13. Consensus resource

The base layer uses proof of work as the Sybil-resistance and history-weighting mechanism.

Required resource properties

The PoW function must:

impose real external cost,

resist trivial outsourcing of all advantage to a tiny specialized hardware cartel,

remain efficiently verifiable by every full node,

and preserve public measurability of accumulated work.

Open variable

The specific PoW family remains open:

memory-hard,

CPU-biased,

rotating family,

or other.

That decision moves to Specification 02.

14. Core ledger geometry
14.1 Data structure

The base ledger is a directed acyclic graph of PoW blocks.

Each block references one or more parent blocks from the current frontier.

Each block contains:

a block header,

parent references,

PoW nonce / solution,

transactions,

metadata required for ordering,

and commitment roots required by the private state model.

14.2 DAG invariants

The graph must remain acyclic.

Every valid block must:

reference valid prior blocks,

satisfy PoW,

satisfy block size and proof constraints,

and conform to timestamp / future-skew policy.

15. Canonical ordering

Because the graph is not linear by default, the protocol must define a deterministic total order extension over the partial order.

That ordering is used for:

spend conflict resolution,

final state commitment,

transaction indexing,

light-client proofs,

and confirmation scoring.

15.1 Ordering requirements

The ordering rule must be:

deterministic,

derivable from public data,

independent of privileged observers,

stable under honest parallel block production,

and hard to manipulate cheaply.

15.2 Canonical order output

The consensus engine shall compute:

a preferred block ordering,

a preferred transaction ordering inside finalized scope,

and a cumulative confidence score for each block / transaction.

The exact ordering function remains to be fixed in a later spec, but the requirement is fixed now.

16. Private transaction conflict model

This is the critical bridge between privacy and consensus.

16.1 Public conflict anchors

Although sender, receiver, amount, and linkage are hidden, each spend must reveal a public nullifier or equivalent one-time conflict marker.

A nullifier must be:

unique per spendable note / state,

publicly checkable for duplication,

unlinkable to ownership identity,

and infeasible to predict usefully by third parties.

16.2 Conflict rule

A transaction is invalid if it contains a nullifier already accepted in the canonical finalized order.

If two transactions attempt to spend the same underlying private state and therefore reveal the same nullifier, they form a conflict set.

The consensus engine resolves the conflict by canonical order.

Rule

The earliest canonical valid appearance of a nullifier wins.
All later appearances are rejected as double spends.

This gives the system a clean bridge:

privacy is preserved,

double spends are still machine-detectable,

no validator discretion is needed.

17. Transaction validity at consensus boundary

A transaction is admissible to a block only if:

its proof verifies,

its nullifiers are well-formed,

its commitment and state references are well-formed,

its fee policy is satisfied,

and its serialized size and proof weight are within limits.

A transaction becomes canonically accepted only once it appears in an accepted block and survives conflict and reorg rules.

18. Block validity

A block is valid iff:

all parent references are valid,

PoW satisfies target,

timestamp is within policy bounds,

all transactions are individually admissible,

block-level roots and commitments are correct,

and aggregate resource rules are satisfied.

Invalid blocks are never relayed further once detected.

19. Reorg policy

The system uses probabilistic finality based on accumulated work in the DAG’s preferred order.

Principle

Blocks and transactions gain confidence as more work accumulates in support of their canonical position.

Reorg acceptance

Reorgs are allowed inside the non-final zone.

Reorgs become progressively less acceptable as work accumulates.

Wallet policy tiers

The protocol shall expose at least three practical tiers:

fast provisional acceptance for very low-risk contexts,

merchant acceptance for ordinary commercial contexts,

high-value finality for large transfers and settlements.

The exact thresholds are not fixed here, but the system must expose a confidence model simple enough for real wallet UX.

20. Mempool and conflict handling

The mempool shall support:

multiple candidate blocks,

conflict-set tracking by nullifier,

eviction rules for obviously losing conflicts,

fee-aware selection among non-conflicting candidates,

and privacy-preserving relay behavior.

Important constraint:
the mempool must not turn nullifier conflicts into an easy metadata oracle for observers. That issue moves partly into the network/privacy spec, but consensus has to respect it.

21. Miner incentives

Miners are paid to do three things:

extend the preferred frontier,

include valid fee-paying transactions,

and avoid producing blocks that are likely to lose ordering weight.

Reward components

block subsidy,

transaction fees,

possibly proof-verification-aware fee weight.

Anti-pathology requirements

The incentive model must discourage:

excessive empty-block production,

pathological parent selection,

and profitable manipulation of conflict visibility.

22. Spam and resource control

Consensus must define resource accounting for private transactions.

Because private transactions often carry heavier proofs than transparent ones, admission must consider:

serialized byte size,

proof verification cost,

state access burden,

and nullifier / commitment update burden.

The fee market or admission rules must prevent:

cheap proof spam,

frontier flooding,

and DAG bloat through low-value garbage.

23. Failure modes

The architecture explicitly admits these risks:

23.1 Mining concentration

PoW can centralize into pools.

23.2 Ordering complexity bugs

BlockDAG ordering logic can fail in subtle ways.

23.3 Privacy / consensus interaction bugs

Nullifier handling, proof validity, and canonical ordering can interact badly if underspecified.

23.4 Light-client verification pressure

Parallel history and private state can make light-client correctness harder.

23.5 Confirmation ambiguity

Users can be confused if the confidence model is not brutally simple.

These are not reasons to abandon the design.
They are reasons to force harsher validation before launch.

24. Mandatory validation before final lock

The design cannot be considered locked until it survives:

Formal ordering analysis
Show that canonical ordering is deterministic and conflict-safe.

Double-spend simulation under parallel honest mining
Show that ordinary concurrency does not create pathological conflict ambiguity.

Selfish-mining and withholding simulation
Evaluate profitability under realistic propagation assumptions.

Reorg stress tests
Quantify finality confidence under adverse network conditions.

Proof-heavy spam tests
Ensure resource rules prevent proof-bloat denial tactics.

Light-client verification prototype
Demonstrate that relevant proofs can be checked without catastrophic leakage.

If blockDAG fails these tests, the protocol must downgrade to a linear-chain PoW base.

25. Formal discard triggers

The chosen consensus design is rejected if any of the following becomes true:

canonical order depends on privileged observation;

nullifier conflict handling becomes ambiguous under normal concurrency;

reorg behavior is too hard to explain operationally;

blockDAG complexity materially centralizes implementation knowledge;

privacy proofs and ordering make light clients impractical;

mining incentives favor pathological parent-selection or censorship too easily.

26. Resulting decision

So the formal decision at this stage is:

Chosen base direction

PoW blockDAG with deterministic canonical order and nullifier-based private conflict resolution

Status

Selected provisionally, pending formal and adversarial validation

Fallback

PoW linear chain with the same private-spend conflict model

This is the honest position.

Not “blockDAG is superior.”
Not “linear chain is safer by default.”
But:

blockDAG is the better target if it survives contact with privacy, light clients, and adversarial testing; otherwise it gets cut without sentimentality.

27. What this closes

This specification closes these questions:

the base layer will use PoW, not PoS or visible-validator consensus;

the ledger may be parallelized;

double-spend prevention in a private system will rely on public conflict markers such as nullifiers;

finality will be probabilistic but operationally tiered;

and consensus simplicity remains a hard constraint, not an afterthought.

28. What remains open

This specification leaves open:

the exact PoW function,

the exact canonical ordering formula,

the exact proof system,

the exact light-client query protocol,

fee / admission weighting,

anti-pool mitigation,

and final confirmation thresholds.

Those are the next documents.

The next correct deliverable is:

Specification 02 — PoW Function and Mining Decentralization Strategy

Specification 02 — PoW Function and Mining Decentralization Strategy
1. Purpose

This specification defines the requirements for the proof-of-work function, miner incentive environment, and decentralization strategy of the base layer.

Specification 01 chose a PoW-based sovereign base provisionally.
That was only the first cut.

This document answers the harder question:

what kind of PoW can secure the network without quietly recreating an oligarchy of hardware vendors, industrial miners, pools, and favorable jurisdictions?

This specification does not pretend to solve that perfectly.
It defines the least-bad design target and the tests required before lock-in.

2. Scope

This specification covers:

PoW function requirements,

mining hardware assumptions,

pool formation risks,

miner centralization risks,

issuance-security interaction,

energy and jurisdictional pressure,

anti-cartel goals,

and the acceptance criteria for a PoW family.

It does not define:

the exact proof system for private transactions,

the stable unit,

the disclosure layer,

or wallet key management.

3. Design thesis

A sovereign monetary base needs an externalized security cost.

But not every externalized cost is equally acceptable.

A bad PoW choice can destroy the project in at least five ways:

by collapsing mining into specialized hardware oligopoly;

by forcing normal users and small operators out of participation;

by concentrating hashrate in a few pools;

by making the chain trivially attackable through energy or jurisdictional chokepoints;

by creating such high operational friction that the protocol survives only as a niche cult artifact.

So the target is not “the strongest PoW” in the abstract.
The target is:

a PoW family that keeps consensus capture expensive while delaying, diluting, and exposing centralization pressures as much as possible.

4. Hard requirements

The PoW system must satisfy all of the following.

4.1 External cost

Producing valid blocks must require a measurable external cost.

4.2 Cheap public verification

Every full node must verify work cheaply relative to producing it.

4.3 No dependence on prior wealth

Mining power must not be granted by owning the native asset.

4.4 Friction against ASIC oligopoly

The function should resist rapid domination by a tiny specialized hardware supply chain, or at least make that domination slower, costlier, and more visible.

4.5 High accessibility floor

Commodity hardware participation should remain possible for a meaningful portion of the system lifecycle.

4.6 Predictable security accounting

The network must be able to estimate attack cost through public work metrics.

4.7 Compatibility with private transaction load

The PoW system must coexist with a heavier private-transaction verification environment.

4.8 Governance minimization

The work function must not force frequent discretionary changes as a permanent crutch.

5. Non-goals

The PoW design will not optimize for:

“green” branding,

industrial miner friendliness,

single-device fairness fantasies,

or perfect immunity to pooling.

Those are not realistic goals.

The real goal is more modest and more honest:

make capture harder,

make concentration slower,

make abuse more visible,

and preserve enough accessibility that the network does not become performatively decentralized.

6. Adversary model

The mining layer assumes adversaries that may control:

specialized hardware supply,

cheap electricity,

large capital pools,

mining pool infrastructure,

regulatory influence in friendly jurisdictions,

and coordinated censorship incentives.

The relevant attack classes are:

hashrate cartelization,

selfish mining,

pool capture,

jurisdictional pressure on industrial miners,

selective transaction censorship,

withholding,

supply-chain concentration,

and “soft centralization” through wallet defaults and pool discovery.

7. Candidate PoW families

The serious candidates are these.

A. SHA-style compute-dominant PoW

Strength:

simple, mature, well-understood.

Weakness:

tends toward brutal ASIC specialization and industrial concentration.

Verdict:

rejected as default target.

B. Memory-hard PoW

Strength:

raises the cost of pure compute specialization;

narrows the advantage of custom silicon relative to commodity systems.

Weakness:

does not eliminate specialization forever;

can still centralize through system integration, board design, and supply chain.

Verdict:

preferred candidate family.

C. CPU-biased PoW

Strength:

broad commodity availability;

potentially lower barrier to entry.

Weakness:

still vulnerable to botnets, cloud concentration, memory optimization races, and eventual specialized hardware pressure.

Verdict:

strong subfamily candidate within memory-hard designs.

D. Rotating multi-function PoW

Strength:

complicates single-purpose hardware optimization.

Weakness:

adds verification and implementation complexity;

may create governance dependence if rotation is too discretionary.

Verdict:

possible, but dangerous if it requires frequent human intervention.

E. Hybrid work-resource schemes

Strength:

may combine different scarcity dimensions.

Weakness:

often become complicated without truly improving decentralization.

Verdict:

not preferred unless formal testing proves clear benefit.

8. Provisional decision
Chosen direction

Memory-hard, commodity-hardware-biased PoW, with a strong preference for a CPU-accessible design over a compute-pure or ASIC-friendly design.

Rationale

This gives the best currently defensible trade-off between:

public verifiability,

externalized security cost,

broader access to mining,

and delayed concentration into highly specialized hardware.

This does not mean:

“everyone mines equally,”

“ASICs become impossible forever,”

or “mining stays home-user dominated permanently.”

Those are slogans, not engineering outcomes.

The claim is narrower:

a memory-hard, CPU-accessible PoW is less structurally self-defeating for this system than ASIC-maximalist work functions.

9. What “memory-hard” must mean here

The phrase will not be used loosely.

For this architecture, the PoW function should impose meaningful dependence on:

memory bandwidth,

memory latency,

and memory footprint,

not just arithmetic throughput.

That matters because pure arithmetic specialization is too easy to compress into industrial custom hardware advantages.

A useful memory-hard function should therefore:

reduce the return on pure compute scaling,

make commodity systems relatively less hopeless,

and raise the engineering burden of specialized domination.

10. CPU-accessible does not mean CPU-exclusive

This is a critical design rule.

If the protocol says “CPU only” as if that were enforceable, it is lying.

The real target is:

CPU-accessible,

not instantly GPU- or ASIC-dominated,

and economically broad enough to avoid near-immediate hardware aristocracy.

The system must assume that any valuable PoW will attract optimization.
The design goal is not to ban optimization.
It is to shape the optimization curve.

11. Why ASIC-friendly PoW is rejected

For this project, ASIC-friendly work functions fail too early and too hard.

They tend to produce:

vendor concentration,

geographic concentration,

high capital barrier,

stronger regulatory choke points,

and a culture where ordinary participation becomes symbolic rather than meaningful.

That might be tolerable for an asset whose only goal is “survive as digital gold.”
It is misaligned with a privacy-preserving monetary base that also needs plausible social decentralization.

So the rejection is not moral.
It is architectural.

12. Why memory-hard PoW is still dangerous

This is where honesty matters.

Memory-hard PoW can still fail by:

rewarding board-level integration advantages,

favoring specific memory suppliers,

concentrating around optimized firmware and tuning expertise,

enabling botnet abuse,

and drifting toward semi-specialized hardware ecosystems anyway.

So memory-hard is not a magic bullet.
It is only the least-bad terrain.

13. Pool centralization problem

This problem is unavoidable enough that it must be first-class.

Even with a better PoW function, miners pool because:

payout variance is painful,

uptime is uneven,

and individual miners want smoother returns.

That means the protocol cannot declare victory just because the work function is better.

Required posture

The architecture must treat pool centralization as a core risk and design around it socially, economically, and operationally.

Protocol-adjacent mitigation goals

The system should favor designs that:

reduce the advantage of very large pools,

make censorship patterns visible,

and minimize dependence on a few default endpoints.

This does not guarantee decentralized mining.
It only reduces the speed and comfort of pool oligopoly.

14. Botnet problem

A CPU-accessible PoW invites a different threat:

parasitic mining on compromised commodity machines.

That means the design must not confuse “commodity accessible” with “socially harmless.”

The system must model:

botnet-sourced mining pressure,

hostile cloud rental bursts,

and malware-driven hashrate concentration.

This does not disqualify CPU-accessible PoW.
It means miner-decentralization strategy must include abuse monitoring assumptions and cost modeling.

15. Cloud and hosted concentration

Even if the PoW is commodity-friendly, mining can still centralize through:

hyperscaler compute rentals,

managed hosting,

industrial RAM procurement,

and orchestration advantages.

So the decentralization model must assume:

a meaningful part of apparent “distributed mining” may still be operationally centralized.

That risk must be reflected in network-health metrics.

16. Energy and jurisdictional pressure

Because PoW anchors security in real cost, it inherits energy politics.

The system must assume attacks through:

electricity price asymmetry,

mining bans,

selective enforcement,

customs pressure on hardware,

and restrictions on industrial hosting.

Design implication

The protocol should prefer a mining ecosystem that can survive in fragmented, mixed-scale participation rather than requiring giant energy campuses to stay secure.

This is another reason to avoid a design that is naturally optimized for industrial ASIC farms.

17. Issuance-security interaction

A base-layer PoW system needs a security budget.

That budget comes from:

block subsidy,

transaction fees,

or both.

Required design rule

The issuance model must not assume fees alone will secure the chain early.

Security philosophy

A tail-emission-like logic may be more defensible than hard-zero issuance if zero issuance would cause miner collapse, censorship pressure, or consensus fragility.

This is not yet a final issuance decision.
It is a constraint on the next specification.

18. Compatibility with private transaction load

Private transactions are heavier than transparent ones.

That means mining and validation are already operating in a costlier environment:

larger proofs,

heavier verification,

more complex state commitments.

The PoW design must therefore avoid a consensus resource so specialized or brittle that the additional weight of private settlement makes ordinary node and miner operation unrealistic.

In other words:

the work function cannot be chosen in isolation from proof verification cost.

19. Required miner decentralization strategy

The protocol must pair the PoW function with an explicit decentralization strategy.

That strategy shall include:

19.1 Accessibility target

Define minimum viable commodity hardware participation expectations.

19.2 Concentration monitoring

Track:

pool concentration,

client concentration,

geographic concentration,

and hosted-infrastructure dependence.

19.3 Default software posture

Avoid defaults that silently push miners to a tiny set of pools or infrastructure providers.

19.4 Censorship visibility

Provide tooling to detect and publicize transaction censorship patterns by large mining entities.

19.5 Diversity incentives

Prefer ecosystem choices that reduce monoculture:

multiple mining implementations,

diversified node software,

and distributed relay paths.

20. Formal evaluation criteria for the PoW family

The chosen PoW function must pass all of these categories.

20.1 Verification simplicity

Nodes must verify work cheaply and deterministically.

20.2 Commodity accessibility

Commodity systems must remain nontrivial participants.

20.3 Specialization delay

The function should delay and dilute hardware specialization.

20.4 Abuse resistance

The function should not make botnet or burst-rental attacks trivial relative to honest participation.

20.5 Implementation clarity

Independent teams must be able to implement it correctly.

20.6 Operational stability

The function should not require constant protocol churn to remain viable.

20.7 Integration fitness

It must coexist with a private, proof-heavy transaction layer.

21. Rejected design temptations

These are explicitly rejected.

21.1 “Make it ASIC-proof forever”

Not credible.

21.2 “Just rotate algorithms constantly”

Too governance-heavy unless narrowly bounded and automated.

21.3 “GPU-friendly is good enough”

Too vague; GPU-friendliness alone does not guarantee decentralization.

21.4 “Let the market decide”

That is how centralization gets rebranded as neutrality.

21.5 “Mining decentralization is a social problem, not a protocol problem”

False. It is both.

22. Open implementation directions

The likely implementation directions that survive at this stage are:

Direction A

Single memory-hard, CPU-accessible PoW, carefully tuned and stable.

Pros:

simpler implementation,

simpler verification,

lower governance burden.

Cons:

eventual specialization pressure remains.

Direction B

Bounded rotating family of memory-hard variants, with deterministic schedule and no human discretion.

Pros:

raises long-term specialization cost.

Cons:

more complexity,

harder audit surface,

more failure modes.

Provisional preference

Start with Direction A unless testing shows unavoidable rapid specialization.

23. Formal discard triggers

The selected PoW strategy is rejected if any of the following becomes true in modeling or prototype testing:

commodity participation becomes effectively meaningless too early;

specialized advantage becomes overwhelming within an unacceptably short horizon;

verification burden becomes too high for ordinary full nodes;

botnet or burst-rental abuse dominates honest mining economics;

pool concentration reaches levels that make censorship or reorg abuse too easy;

implementation complexity becomes dependent on a tiny expert priesthood;

or the function interacts badly with private transaction verification at scale.

24. Resulting decision

At this stage, the architecture formally chooses:

Selected PoW strategy

Memory-hard, commodity-hardware-biased PoW

Status

Selected provisionally, pending adversarial evaluation and prototype measurements

Rejected for current purposes

compute-dominant ASIC-maximalist PoW,

stake-based validity,

discretionary algorithm churn as a default defense.

25. What this closes

This specification closes these questions:

the base layer will not use ASIC-friendly-first PoW as the target;

the mining strategy must explicitly value broader accessibility;

pool centralization is treated as a design problem, not an afterthought;

the issuance model must preserve a real security budget;

and the PoW choice must be validated against the needs of a private, proof-heavy monetary base.

26. What remains open

This specification leaves open:

the exact memory-hard function,

its parameterization,

the issuance curve,

pool-mitigation tactics,

abuse thresholds,

and how mining telemetry can be observed without creating surveillance incentives.

The next correct deliverable is:

Specification 03 — Private Transaction Model, Nullifiers, Commitments, and Supply Correctness

continue
Thought for 6s
Specification 03 — Private Transaction Model, Nullifiers, Commitments, and Supply Correctness
1. Purpose

This specification defines the private transaction model of the base layer.

It answers the next hard question after consensus and PoW:

how can the system hide sender, receiver, amount, and graph structure while still preventing double spend, preserving supply correctness, and remaining verifiable by independent nodes?

This document covers:

private notes / state objects,

commitments,

nullifiers,

spend authorization,

hidden-value conservation,

mint / issuance accounting,

burn / destruction accounting,

and failure conditions.

It does not yet finalize:

the exact proof system,

the exact encryption scheme for wallet scanning,

or the full light-client protocol.

2. Design thesis

A private monetary system fails if it chooses any of these two extremes:

transparent validity that makes all balances and graphs visible;

opaque black-box validity that hides everything so well that no one can detect inflation, broken state transitions, or malformed spends.

The design target is narrower and harsher:

hide what must be hidden, expose only what is required for machine-valid conflict detection and supply correctness, and make everything else provable without revealing financial life.

That means the transaction model needs four pillars:

private state representation

public one-time conflict markers

proof of conservation and authorization

publicly auditable issuance accounting

3. Scope

This specification covers:

the form of private money objects,

the spend lifecycle,

note creation and destruction,

double-spend prevention via nullifiers,

transaction validity conditions,

value conservation,

issuance accounting,

fee handling,

and state commitment rules.

4. Non-goals

This specification does not attempt to provide:

perfect endpoint privacy,

human-readable payment metadata,

stable-unit semantics,

or legal disclosure policy beyond the primitive hooks needed for later layers.

5. Core architecture choice

The base layer shall use a private note model.

A note is the fundamental private spendable unit.

Each note represents hidden value under hidden ownership and is committed to the chain without publicly exposing:

owner identity,

amount,

linkage to previous notes,

or recipient identity.

The system shall not use a transparent account-balance model for the base monetary asset.

6. Note model
6.1 Note contents

Each note shall contain, conceptually:

asset identifier,

value,

recipient ownership material,

note randomness / blinding,

creation context reference,

and optional encrypted recovery / scanning payload.

Some of these fields are never public in plaintext.

6.2 Public representation

The chain stores a commitment to the note, not the note in plaintext.

The commitment must be:

binding,

hiding,

deterministic from note contents plus randomness,

and efficient to verify within proofs.

6.3 Spendability

A note becomes spendable when:

its commitment has entered canonically accepted state,

any maturity / lock rules are satisfied,

and the spender can prove ownership authorization.

7. Commitment model
7.1 Commitment requirements

The note commitment scheme must satisfy:

hiding of value and owner,

collision resistance,

binding to note contents,

compatibility with membership proofs,

efficient inclusion into state trees or accumulators.

7.2 Commitment set

All live or historically relevant note commitments are represented in a commitment structure used by consensus and proofs.

This structure must support:

insertion of newly created notes,

proof that input notes existed,

and proof that spends refer to valid prior state.

7.3 Canonical state root

Each accepted block shall commit to the current canonical private-state root or equivalent accumulator state.

That root anchors:

note existence,

proof verification context,

and future spend references.

8. Nullifier model
8.1 Purpose

A nullifier is a public one-time conflict marker derived from a spendable note and the spender’s authorization material.

It exists for one reason:

make double spending publicly machine-detectable without revealing the underlying note details.

8.2 Nullifier requirements

A nullifier must be:

unique for each spendable note,

deterministic for the legitimate spend path,

infeasible for outsiders to precompute usefully,

infeasible to invert into note identity or owner identity,

and cheap for nodes to check for duplication.

8.3 Visibility

Nullifiers are public.

That is not a bug.
That is the minimal public leak required to prevent hidden double spending without discretionary validators.

8.4 Conflict rule

If two transactions contain the same nullifier, they conflict.

Consensus resolves the conflict by canonical order.
The earliest canonically accepted valid spend wins.
All later appearances of that nullifier are invalid.

9. Spend authorization
9.1 Ownership proof

To spend a note, the transaction must include proof that the spender is authorized to consume that note.

Authorization must not reveal:

the public identity of the owner,

the note plaintext,

or the graph linkage beyond what is minimally necessary.

9.2 Authorization properties

The authorization system must prove simultaneously:

the spender knows valid spending authority,

the note exists in committed state,

the note has not already been spent in canonical state,

and the transaction obeys conservation rules.

9.3 No interactive validator trust

Spend authorization must not require any validator, coordinator, mint, or remote service to approve ownership.

10. Transaction structure

A base private transaction shall include, conceptually:

one or more input-note references via proof context,

one or more nullifiers,

one or more output note commitments,

a proof object,

fee declaration,

and optional encrypted payloads for recipient discovery or later selective disclosure hooks.

The chain does not expose plaintext sender, receiver, or amounts.

11. Transaction validity conditions

A transaction is valid iff all of the following hold:

the proof verifies;

every referenced input note is a valid committed prior state under the canonical state root;

every nullifier is well-formed;

no nullifier has already appeared in finalized canonical state;

output commitments are well-formed;

hidden-value conservation holds;

fees are non-negative and valid under policy;

issuance rules are obeyed;

asset-type rules are obeyed;

serialization and proof resource limits are respected.

If any condition fails, the transaction is invalid.

12. Hidden-value conservation

This is one of the most dangerous parts of the system.

Because amounts are hidden, nodes cannot simply sum visible inputs and outputs.

So the proof system must establish:

total hidden input value = total hidden output value + fee + authorized mint/burn delta

This must be proven without revealing the amounts themselves.

12.1 Conservation rule

For ordinary transfers:

sum(inputs) = sum(outputs) + fee

For issuance or authorized minting paths:

sum(inputs) + authorized issuance = sum(outputs) + fee

For burns or destruction:

sum(inputs) = sum(outputs) + fee + burned amount

12.2 Unauthorized creation rejection

Any transaction that would create hidden value outside authorized issuance rules must fail proof verification.

13. Fees
13.1 Fee visibility

The system must choose between:

publicly visible fee amount,

hidden fee amount with provable accounting,

or partially visible fee metadata.

Provisional preference:
fees may be visible or more exposed than transfer values if that materially simplifies resource accounting and does not destroy the system’s privacy objective.

The key principle is:

the system protects financial graph privacy first;
it does not sacrifice supply correctness for aesthetic purity.

13.2 Fee validity

Every transaction must prove that fee obligations are met.
Consensus must be able to account for fees in block reward logic.

14. Issuance model hooks

This document does not finalize issuance curve, but it does define how issuance appears in the private transaction system.

14.1 Authorized issuance path

New value may enter the system only through explicitly authorized issuance transactions or block reward logic.

Those paths must be provable and publicly accounted for.

14.2 Public issuance accounting

Even if individual coin creation outputs are privately committed, the total issuance schedule must remain publicly auditable.

This requires:

public rules for block subsidy,

public accounting of cumulative authorized issuance,

proof-enforced adherence inside each issuance event.

14.3 No silent inflation

The system must make it impossible, or at minimum independently detectable, for arbitrary hidden-value creation to occur without violating public issuance accounting or proof soundness.

15. Burn and destruction hooks

The architecture must also support provable destruction of value for cases such as:

supply contraction mechanisms,

bridgeless settlement abstractions,

or stable-layer collateral retirement.

Burn paths must be:

explicit,

provable,

publicly accounted for in aggregate,

and never confused with accidental loss.

16. State transition model

The base private state evolves as follows:

canonical state root at block N is known;

a transaction proves membership of its input notes in valid prior committed state;

the transaction reveals nullifiers for consumed notes;

the transaction produces new output commitments;

consensus accepts the transaction if proof, nullifier, and policy checks pass;

the next canonical state root reflects note consumption and note creation.

This means the state transition function is not:

“update transparent balances,”
but rather:

“consume hidden notes via nullifiers and create new hidden commitments under proof.”

17. Multi-asset policy

The base model must decide whether:

the base layer supports one asset only at genesis,

or supports multiple hidden asset types natively.

Provisional preference:
start with single native monetary asset at the base privacy model, with architecture extensibility for future asset identifiers.

Reason:
multi-asset privacy increases complexity in:

conservation proofs,

fee accounting,

light-client scanning,

and disclosure semantics.

The base asset must be solved first.

18. Range and validity constraints

Because amounts are hidden, the system must prevent invalid values such as:

negative values,

overflow / underflow values,

out-of-range amounts,

malformed asset tags.

Therefore the proof system must enforce:

value range constraints,

asset consistency constraints,

and conservation constraints.

If not, hidden arithmetic becomes an inflation bug waiting to happen.

19. Recipient discovery hooks

A private chain still needs recipients to discover notes meant for them.

This specification does not finalize the exact recipient discovery method, but it requires:

recipients must be able to discover spendable notes addressed to them,

without requiring transparent addresses,

and without forcing catastrophic leakage to remote scanners.

This remains one of the key blockers for the light-client spec.

20. Privacy leakage budget

No private transaction model is perfectly silent.

The design must explicitly budget acceptable public exposure.

At this stage, allowed public exposure is limited to:

nullifiers,

output commitments,

proof objects,

block inclusion,

resource accounting fields,

and whatever minimal metadata consensus needs.

Not allowed as default public exposure:

sender identity,

receiver identity,

amount,

balance graph,

transaction counterparties,

or reusable public account history.

21. Supply correctness model

This is the second critical pillar after double-spend prevention.

A private monetary system must answer:

how can outsiders verify that total money supply follows protocol rules even if transaction values are hidden?

The system’s answer shall be:

every transaction proves hidden-value conservation;

all issuance events are explicitly authorized by public protocol rules;

cumulative issuance is publicly derivable from protocol state;

burns are explicitly modeled and publicly aggregated;

proof soundness becomes part of monetary soundness.

21.1 Publicly auditable totals

Observers must be able to derive:

total protocol-authorized issuance to date,

total explicit protocol-authorized burns to date,

and therefore the expected aggregate circulating supply.

This aggregate accounting must not require revealing individual user balances.

21.2 Independent auditability

An independent implementation must be able to verify:

block reward correctness,

transaction proof correctness,

aggregate issuance progression,

aggregate fee accounting,

and state-root consistency.

If only the reference client can do that reliably, the design fails.

22. Failure modes

The private transaction model must openly admit its main failure risks.

22.1 Proof soundness failure

If the proof system is unsound, hidden inflation becomes possible.

22.2 Nullifier collision or misuse

If nullifier derivation is flawed, double-spend detection may fail or privacy may degrade.

22.3 Commitment misuse

If commitments are malformed or binding assumptions fail, state integrity can break.

22.4 Range-check failure

If hidden values are not range-constrained correctly, overflow-style inflation becomes possible.

22.5 State-root inconsistency

If the state commitment logic is underspecified, clients can diverge on valid history.

22.6 Recipient discovery leakage

If note discovery requires naive remote scanning, privacy collapses operationally even if the chain itself is private.

23. Mandatory validation before final lock

This transaction model cannot be considered final until it survives:

formal proof of nullifier uniqueness and conflict soundness

formal proof of hidden-value conservation correctness

range-check and overflow adversarial testing

issuance-accounting conformance testing across independent implementations

recipient-discovery privacy modeling

reorg interaction tests with private spends and nullifier conflicts

light-client feasibility prototype

If any of these fail, the model must be revised before implementation lock.

24. Formal discard triggers

This private transaction model is rejected if any of the following becomes true:

nullifier-based conflict resolution is ambiguous under canonical ordering;

hidden-value conservation cannot be independently audited with confidence;

issuance accounting cannot be derived publicly in aggregate;

the proof system requires unreasonable verifier cost for full nodes;

recipient discovery inevitably forces unacceptable privacy leakage;

or implementation complexity makes independent review unrealistic.

25. Resulting decisions

This specification closes these questions:

the base asset uses a private note model;

private state is represented by commitments, not transparent balances;

double-spend prevention uses public nullifiers;

spend authorization is proven, not validator-approved;

hidden-value conservation is mandatory;

aggregate supply must remain publicly auditable even though individual values are hidden;

and issuance / burn paths must be explicit protocol objects.

26. What remains open

This specification leaves open:

the exact proof system,

the exact commitment construction,

the exact nullifier derivation scheme,

the exact state-tree / accumulator design,

the exact fee-visibility policy,

and the exact recipient-discovery mechanism.

Those move to the next documents.

The next correct deliverable is:

Specification 04 — State Commitments, Accumulators, and Light-Client Architecture

continue
Thought for a couple of seconds
Specification 04 — State Commitments, Accumulators, and Light-Client Architecture
1. Purpose

This specification defines how the system commits to private state, proves note existence and spendability, and supports light clients without collapsing privacy.

This is one of the most dangerous parts of the design.

A private base layer can look sound on paper and still fail in practice because of one ugly fact:

users do not all run full nodes, and the moment a light client depends naively on remote scanning, indexing, or wallet-assisted query patterns, privacy starts leaking operationally even if the chain is private.

So this document is about three things at once:

canonical private state commitment,

efficient membership and update proofs,

and light-client operation under hostile assumptions.

2. Scope

This specification covers:

state commitment structure,

note insertion and spent-state tracking,

nullifier set representation,

historical and current-state proof requirements,

light-client synchronization model,

remote-query minimization,

wallet discovery constraints,

and verification responsibilities.

It does not finalize:

the exact proof system internals,

the exact transport mixnet,

or the selective-disclosure layer.

3. Design thesis

The private-state layer must satisfy two goals that naturally fight each other:

full nodes need a canonical, auditable state model;

light clients need usable access without revealing their financial life to whoever serves them.

The architecture therefore rejects both extremes:

full-node absolutism, where normal users are effectively excluded;

remote-indexer dependence, where wallets become privacy theater.

The design target is:

full nodes remain the source of truth; light clients verify enough locally to avoid blind trust; and the query model is shaped so remote services learn as little as possible.

4. Core requirements
4.1 Canonical private-state commitment

The chain must commit to a deterministic representation of private monetary state.

4.2 Efficient membership proofs

A spender must prove that an input note existed in valid committed state.

4.3 Efficient nullifier lookup

Nodes must detect duplicate nullifiers cheaply and deterministically.

4.4 Reorg-safe state transitions

State commitment logic must remain well-defined under non-final reorgs.

4.5 Light-client viability

A non-full-node client must be able to discover its notes and verify relevant state without handing a remote server a clean reconstruction of wallet activity.

4.6 Bounded verifier burden

Full-node verification must remain affordable enough that the private chain does not centralize around a few heroic operators.

5. State model overview

The private monetary base maintains two canonical state domains:

A. Note commitment state

Tracks committed notes that have been created by accepted transactions.

B. Nullifier state

Tracks one-time public conflict markers for spent notes.

These two domains are logically separate even if an implementation compresses or co-structures them.

That separation matters because they answer different questions:

note state answers: “did this spendable object exist?”

nullifier state answers: “has this object already been consumed?”

6. State commitment architecture
6.1 Commitment root

Every accepted block shall commit to a canonical state root or equivalent accumulator digest that reflects:

note insertions from accepted outputs,

nullifier insertions from accepted spends,

and any authorized issuance / burn effects that alter aggregate state.

This root must be:

deterministic,

reproducible from public chain data,

verifiable by independent implementations,

and stable under the canonical ordering defined in the consensus spec.

6.2 Two-root preference

Provisional preference:
use separate commitments for:

note commitment state,

nullifier state.

Possible later extension:
add an auxiliary root for issuance / aggregate accounting if that simplifies auditability.

Reason:
forcing all concerns into one monolithic structure may create avoidable complexity and poorer implementation clarity.

7. Note commitment structure
7.1 Functional requirements

The note commitment structure must support:

insertion of new output commitments,

proof that a note exists in committed history or active state,

efficient verification of membership proofs,

compatibility with private transaction proofs,

and deterministic reconstruction by full nodes.

7.2 Candidate structure families

The realistic candidate families are:

Merkle-tree variants,

append-only commitment trees,

accumulator-based structures,

frontier-friendly tree variants for parallel block production.

7.3 Provisional preference

Use a tree or accumulator model that supports:

append-like insertion semantics,

compact membership proofs,

and deterministic roots that remain manageable under blockDAG ordering.

The precise structure remains open because it must be chosen together with:

proof system,

blockDAG ordering mechanics,

and light-client synchronization model.

8. Nullifier state structure
8.1 Purpose

The nullifier structure exists only to answer:

has this one-time public spend marker already been seen in canonical state?

8.2 Functional requirements

It must support:

efficient insertion,

efficient duplicate detection,

deterministic reconstruction,

reorg-safe rollback or recomputation,

and cheap full-node verification.

8.3 Design rule

The nullifier structure should optimize for fast conflict detection, not for private wallet scanning.

That is a critical separation of concerns.

9. Historical state and spend proofs

A private spend must prove all of the following against canonical state:

the input note commitment existed,

the spend authorization is valid,

the note value is correctly handled under conservation rules,

and the note’s nullifier is the correct one-time public spend marker.

That means the transaction proof must bind to a valid state root or proof context.

The exact binding method remains open, but the rule is fixed:

no spend may be validated against fuzzy or wallet-local state assumptions.

10. Active state vs historical state

This is an important architectural choice.

There are two broad models:

Model A — historical existence proof only

A spender proves that the note existed, and nullifier uniqueness handles spentness.

Model B — active-state membership proof

A spender proves that the note is in an explicitly maintained active set.

Provisional preference:
start from historical existence + nullifier uniqueness, unless performance or proof-size analysis proves active-state membership is clearly superior.

Reason:
maintaining an explicit active set can become costly and subtle under high private-state churn, especially in a parallelized ledger.

11. BlockDAG interaction

Because the consensus target is provisionally PoW blockDAG, state commitment cannot assume a single naive linear append path.

The architecture must specify:

how parallel honest blocks contribute to provisional state,

how canonical ordering collapses provisional branches into a definitive state sequence,

how transactions referencing nearby provisional state behave,

and how reorged branches affect light clients and full nodes.

Design rule

Only canonicalized block order defines committed state.

Provisional branch data may be observed, but wallets and light clients must treat it as non-final until confidence thresholds are met.

12. Light-client problem statement

A light client needs to answer three questions:

did a note intended for me appear on-chain?

is a note I care about included in canonical state?

has a note I spent or received reached meaningful finality?

If the client answers those by asking a remote server directly in a wallet-identifying way, privacy collapses.

So the light-client architecture must minimize:

address-linked queries,

note-linked queries,

scan requests that identify wallet scope,

timing leakage tied to specific payments,

and dependence on one indexer.

13. Rejected light-client models

These models are rejected as defaults.

13.1 Blind-trust remote wallet

A server tells the wallet what it owns, and the wallet mostly trusts it.

Rejected because it collapses sovereignty and privacy.

13.2 Naive remote scanning

The wallet hands a remote party enough information to scan the chain specifically for that wallet’s notes.

Rejected because the remote party can reconstruct wallet activity too easily.

13.3 Mandatory centralized indexer

Rejected because it creates a surveillance chokepoint and an availability chokepoint.

14. Light-client design target

The light client should satisfy this narrower goal:

it may rely on remote data transport, but not on remote truth or wallet-activity revelation.

That means the client should:

verify relevant proofs locally,

request data in a way that does not trivially identify its wallet,

tolerate multiple remote providers,

and preserve a path to stronger privacy when paired with user-run or trusted-community infrastructure.

15. Light-client responsibilities

A light client must be able to do all of the following.

A. Verify chain / DAG headers or equivalent compact consensus data

So it is not blindly trusting history.

B. Verify inclusion or membership proofs for relevant notes

So it is not trusting an indexer’s word about note existence.

C. Verify nullifier-relevant state for its own spends where needed

So it can understand spentness and confirmations.

D. Maintain wallet-local secret material

Without outsourcing spend authority.

E. Distinguish provisional from final state

So merchant and user UX can map to probabilistic finality.

16. Remote data provider model

The system may use remote providers for bandwidth and convenience, but they must be treated as untrusted data sources.

Providers may serve:

compact chain data,

state commitment roots,

note candidate batches,

proof witnesses where safe,

and synchronization metadata.

Providers must not be assumed to know:

the wallet’s full note set,

the user’s identity,

or the user’s exact transaction graph.

The architecture should assume some providers are malicious or surveilling.

17. Wallet note discovery

This remains one of the biggest unsolved operational risks.

The system needs a recipient-discovery method that lets wallets identify notes intended for them without making note discovery itself a privacy leak.

The architecture requirement is:

note discovery must be recipient-possible,

server-ambiguous,

and locally verifiable.

That does not yet define the exact mechanism, but it defines the target.

18. Candidate note-discovery approaches

This spec does not lock one yet, but it defines the candidate space.

Approach A — local full-chain scanning

Best privacy if user runs own full infrastructure.
Worst usability for ordinary users.

Approach B — remote candidate retrieval with local trial decryption / detection

Better usability, but risks server-side clustering if retrieval is too targeted.

Approach C — privacy-preserving query schemes

Potentially better, but can be complex or bandwidth-heavy.

Approach D — delegated scanning with bounded knowledge

Potentially useful for community-run infrastructure, but must not become hidden custodial surveillance.

Provisional preference:
pursue remote candidate retrieval with local verification, but only if the request pattern can be made sufficiently ambiguous.

19. Multi-provider synchronization

Light clients should support querying multiple providers.

Reason:
one provider can lie, censor, or profile.

A multi-provider model helps against:

omission attacks,

censorship attacks,

partial falsification,

and correlation by any single provider.

This does not eliminate surveillance, but it raises cost and uncertainty for the adversary.

20. Proof responsibilities of light clients

A light client must not just “receive wallet events.”
It must verify enough evidence to reject lies.

At minimum, a light client must verify:

consensus header chain / DAG compact validity,

state root continuity,

membership proofs for relevant note commitments,

and finality status under wallet policy.

If that turns out too expensive for practical devices, the system has not yet solved its client model.

21. Reorg handling for light clients

Light clients must operate under the assumption that:

some recent state is provisional,

some note appearances may be reorged out,

and some spentness interpretations may change before deeper confidence is reached.

Therefore the light client must maintain:

provisional state,

confident state,

finalized-enough state under wallet policy.

This is not optional.
Pretending reorg complexity does not exist only pushes the confusion onto users later.

22. Privacy leakage budget for light clients

The design must explicitly limit acceptable leakage.

Not acceptable as default behavior:

server learns the wallet’s full note set;

server learns exact transaction counterparties;

server learns exact amounts;

server can trivially correlate every sync event to a specific wallet payment.

Potentially acceptable if bounded and mitigated:

server sees broad sync windows,

server sees ambiguous candidate-fetch patterns,

server knows the wallet is active without knowing its entire activity graph.

This is still leakage.
It is simply leakage we may have to tolerate temporarily if the alternative is no usable light client at all.

23. Full-node responsibilities

A full node must be able to reconstruct and verify:

canonical state roots,

note commitment insertions,

nullifier insertions,

issuance / burn aggregate accounting,

transaction proof validity,

and reorg correctness.

A full node is the truth anchor of the system.

If the system becomes so heavy that only a few actors can run honest full nodes, the privacy story collapses socially even if it survives cryptographically.

24. Storage classes

The architecture should support multiple storage profiles.

A. Archival full node

Stores all relevant historical data needed for full reconstruction and audit.

B. Validating full node

Stores enough state and history to validate new blocks and serve proofs, without necessarily preserving all raw historical artifacts forever.

C. Light client

Stores compact consensus data, relevant wallet-local data, and proof material needed for self-verification.

This distinction matters because the system must not force every participant into archival behavior.

25. Pruning and state retention

A private chain will grow.
So pruning policy must be explicit.

Pruning must not break:

independent validation,

supply auditability,

light-client proof generation,

or reorg-safe operation inside the accepted horizon.

The exact pruning design remains open, but the rule is fixed:

pruning may reduce storage, but it must not turn validation into faith.

26. Formal interface between consensus and state

Consensus and state logic must remain clearly separated.

Consensus is responsible for:

canonical ordering,

block acceptance,

reorg handling,

and finality scoring.

State logic is responsible for:

commitment updates,

nullifier updates,

proof context interpretation,

and state-root derivation.

This separation is mandatory because otherwise blockDAG complexity and private-state complexity will tangle into an implementation swamp.

27. Failure modes

This architecture openly admits these failure risks.

27.1 Light-client privacy failure

The chain is private, but wallet sync leaks too much.

27.2 State-root divergence

Independent nodes compute different roots under edge-case ordering or reorg conditions.

27.3 Proof-serving centralization

A few infrastructure providers become de facto mandatory for wallet operation.

27.4 Reorg confusion

Wallets mis-handle provisional vs confirmed note state.

27.5 Pruning-induced trust drift

Nodes prune too aggressively and light clients become dependent on trusted history providers.

27.6 BlockDAG/state interaction bugs

Parallel block acceptance interacts badly with note and nullifier updates.

28. Mandatory validation before final lock

This specification cannot be considered locked until it survives:

state-root determinism testing across independent implementations

reorg and parallel-order state-update simulation

light-client sync privacy modeling

multi-provider omission and falsification tests

proof size / bandwidth feasibility on constrained devices

pruning and recovery testing

wallet UX tests for provisional vs final state

If these fail, the architecture must be simplified before continuing.

29. Formal discard triggers

This state and light-client design is rejected if any of the following becomes true:

the light client must reveal near-complete wallet activity to function;

state-root derivation is too complex to implement independently with confidence;

blockDAG ordering and state updates create ambiguity;

pruning breaks independent validation;

or proof-serving becomes so centralized that practical sovereignty disappears.

30. Resulting decisions

This specification closes these questions:

the base layer uses explicit canonical state commitments;

note and nullifier state are treated as distinct concerns;

full nodes are the truth anchor;

light clients must verify locally enough to reject lies;

remote providers are treated as untrusted transport, not trusted truth;

multi-provider sync is required in the design target;

and wallet note discovery is recognized as a first-class blocker, not a minor implementation detail.

31. What remains open

This specification leaves open:

the exact tree / accumulator structure,

the exact note-discovery mechanism,

the exact compact header / DAG summary format,

the exact proof-fetch protocol,

and the exact pruning horizon.

The next correct deliverable is:

Specification 05 — Network Privacy, Propagation, Spy-Node Resistance, and Mobile Operation

continue
Thought for 8s
Specification 05 — Network Privacy, Propagation, Spy-Node Resistance, and Mobile Operation
1. Purpose

This specification defines how the system protects users at the network layer.

That means it addresses the question the ledger alone cannot solve:

how does the system prevent observers, hostile peers, and infrastructure intermediaries from inferring who originated a transaction, when it was likely created, which wallet is active, and how wallet behavior clusters over time?

This document covers:

transaction propagation,

peer selection,

anti-correlation design,

spy-node resistance,

eclipse resistance,

mobile operation,

degraded-network behavior,

and the privacy-performance trade space.

It does not finalize:

the exact light-client note discovery method,

the stable layer,

or the disclosure layer.

2. Design thesis

A private ledger without private transport is incomplete.

If the network layer leaks:

source IP,

propagation timing,

client fingerprint,

sync rhythm,

or wallet query behavior,

then the chain may remain cryptographically private while users become operationally transparent.

So this layer must not be treated as an optional enhancement.

The target is not “perfect anonymity.”
The target is narrower and more honest:

make origin inference, wallet clustering, and transaction correlation materially harder, noisier, less reliable, and more expensive.

3. Scope

This specification covers:

peer topology assumptions,

transaction broadcast flow,

message shaping,

relay strategy,

anti-spy-node design,

anti-eclipse rules,

mobile wallet networking posture,

privacy degradation under weak connectivity,

and operator-visible failure conditions.

4. Non-goals

This layer does not promise:

immunity against a total global passive observer with arbitrary endpoint compromise;

immunity against a fully compromised operating system;

immunity against physical device seizure;

or zero-latency propagation.

Any design that promises those without qualifiers is lying.

5. Core requirements
5.1 No plain-origin gossip as the default

The protocol must not assume that the wallet simply sends a transaction to a few peers and hopes privacy survives.

5.2 Transaction origin ambiguity

A remote observer should not trivially distinguish:

originator,

relay,

re-broadcaster,

or delayed propagator.

5.3 Timing de-correlation

The protocol must reduce the reliability of timing-based inference.

5.4 Spy-node resistance

A hostile peer set must not be able to cluster wallet behavior trivially from repeated contact patterns.

5.5 Eclipse resistance

It must be difficult for an attacker to isolate a node or wallet into a malicious peer view.

5.6 Mobile viability

The privacy posture must survive on mobile devices with intermittent connectivity and constrained resources.

5.7 Degraded-mode honesty

When privacy degrades under poor network conditions, the system must degrade explicitly, not silently.

6. Adversary model

The network layer assumes adversaries that may control:

some network peers,

public RPC endpoints,

consumer ISPs,

hostile Wi-Fi networks,

cloud infrastructure,

traffic-observation points,

app-distribution channels,

and some mobile carrier visibility.

The design considers these attack classes:

transaction origin tracing,

timing correlation,

wallet activity clustering,

eclipse attack,

spy-node graphing,

message fingerprinting,

sync-pattern deanonymization,

censorship by omission,

and mobile reconnection profiling.

7. Rejected defaults

These defaults are explicitly rejected.

7.1 Plain gossip broadcast

Rejected because earliest-seen analysis remains too useful.

7.2 Single trusted gateway

Rejected because it centralizes surveillance and censorship.

7.3 “Use Tor yourself”

Rejected as a complete answer because optional privacy is weak privacy.

7.4 Wallet-specific sync patterns

Rejected because they make remote inference too easy.

7.5 Static long-lived peer sets

Rejected because they invite clustering and eclipse risk.

8. Network architecture target

The architecture target is a privacy-aware relay network with these properties:

transactions do not propagate directly in a way that strongly identifies the originator;

wallets can use relay/privacy infrastructure by default;

peers are selected and rotated in a way that resists observation concentration;

propagation includes mechanisms that reduce deterministic timing fingerprints;

and mobile clients can operate through privacy-preserving relays without needing to expose a stable network identity.

This does not require a single global mixnet in the strictest sense, but it does require mixnet-like privacy properties at the transport layer.

9. Propagation model
9.1 Two-stage propagation principle

Provisional preferred model:

private stem phase
a transaction first travels through a privacy-preserving relay path or staged forwarding path;

diffusion phase
only after staged propagation does it enter broader network dissemination.

This is conceptually similar to the idea that origin hiding and broad availability should not happen in the same first step.

9.2 Design goal

The first nodes that see a transaction should not reliably know whether they are:

close to the origin,

several hops away,

or just seeing a delayed or mixed relay.

10. Relay-path privacy
10.1 Requirements

A privacy-preserving relay path must aim to provide:

source ambiguity,

path variability,

delay variability,

replay-resistant message handling,

and low-value metadata leakage.

10.2 Provisional rule

Transactions should, by default, enter the network through a relay/privacy layer before broad diffusion.

That privacy layer may be:

protocol-native relay staging,

bundled relay over privacy-preserving paths,

or a mixnet-like forwarding layer.

The exact mechanism remains open.

10.3 Hard constraint

It must not require the user to manually compose several tools just to avoid obvious deanonymization.

11. Timing de-correlation

Timing is one of the biggest leaks in practice.

So the network layer must introduce bounded uncertainty in:

first-hop timing,

relay timing,

re-broadcast timing,

and sync request cadence.

This does not mean random delays everywhere without discipline.
That would destroy usability.

The target is more disciplined:

enough timing noise to reduce inference confidence, not so much that the network becomes unusable.

12. Peer selection model
12.1 Peer diversity requirement

Nodes and clients must avoid over-dependence on:

one ASN,

one hosting provider,

one jurisdiction,

one wallet vendor’s bootstrap list,

or one gateway class.

12.2 Rotation requirement

Peer sets must rotate over time.
Static relationships increase clustering risk.

12.3 Admission posture

Peer selection should favor diversity and anti-clustering heuristics, not just raw latency.

Principle

The fastest peers are not necessarily the safest peers.

13. Spy-node resistance
13.1 Problem

A spy node does not need to break cryptography.
It only needs to observe:

when you connect,

what kind of traffic you request,

how often you sync,

which candidate data you ask for,

and whether your behavior repeats.

13.2 Required defenses

The system should reduce spy-node usefulness through:

peer diversity,

rotation,

request batching,

ambiguity in wallet-relevant fetches,

relay-path separation from wallet identity,

and minimized wallet-specific request shapes.

13.3 Hard rule

No component should assume that “most peers are honest enough” is sufficient protection for privacy.

It may be enough for liveness.
It is not enough for anonymity.

14. Eclipse resistance
14.1 Problem

If an attacker can surround a node or light client with malicious peers, the attacker can:

filter visibility,

distort confirmation perception,

delay note discovery,

correlate activity,

or manipulate relay behavior.

14.2 Required defenses

The architecture must include:

diversified peer sourcing,

anti-monoculture bootstrap,

peer rotation,

independent cross-checking,

and stale-view detection.

14.3 Wallet rule

A wallet must not treat a single provider’s view of chain state or pending activity as final truth.

15. Message fingerprint resistance

Different wallets and nodes often betray themselves through tiny behavioral signatures.

The network layer must aim to reduce fingerprinting by standardizing or constraining:

message ordering,

retry timing,

sync burst shape,

relay behavior,

and request envelope patterns.

The goal is not to erase all implementation diversity.
The goal is to avoid privacy failure through gratuitous uniqueness.

16. Light-client network posture

A light client is more exposed than a full node.

So the light-client network posture must assume:

constrained battery,

constrained bandwidth,

intermittent connectivity,

frequent IP changes,

and higher dependence on remote providers.

The design must therefore make privacy-preserving behavior the default, not an expert mode.

Requirements

A light client should:

avoid directly identifying wallet scope to one provider,

support multiple providers,

separate relay path from wallet identity where possible,

batch or generalize requests,

and tolerate higher latency in exchange for better privacy.

17. Mobile operation
17.1 Design philosophy

Mobile is not a second-class afterthought.
If privacy only works well on desktops running full nodes, the system fails socially.

17.2 Mobile requirements

The mobile networking model must support:

background-safe synchronization,

intermittent connectivity,

limited storage,

low-to-moderate bandwidth,

privacy-preserving relay usage,

and explicit handling of low-confidence states.

17.3 Battery and latency trade-off

A mobile device cannot behave like a permanently connected server.

So the protocol must balance:

relay delays,

sync frequency,

and privacy batching,

without making the wallet feel dead or constantly draining power.

18. Degraded-network modes

The system must define explicit degraded modes.

A. normal privacy mode

Relay/privacy path available, multi-provider sync healthy.

B. reduced-privacy mode

Connectivity is weak or relay infrastructure is partially unavailable.

C. emergency-connectivity mode

The wallet can still function, but with clear warnings that metadata privacy is materially weakened.

This is essential.

The user must never be tricked into thinking the wallet is in its strongest privacy mode when it is not.

19. Connectivity censorship and hostile networks

The architecture must assume environments where:

relay traffic is throttled,

some transport types are blocked,

providers disappear,

or privacy infrastructure is selectively censored.

So the system needs:

transport agility,

fallback routing strategies,

and graceful degradation.

It must be harder to silence than a single network pattern or gateway family.

20. Multi-provider model

A node or wallet should be able to draw data from multiple providers and relay paths.

This protects against:

censorship,

omission,

targeted lying,

and unilateral profiling.

It does not eliminate correlation completely.
It does raise attacker cost and reduce single-point visibility.

The architecture should therefore assume multi-provider capability is standard, not advanced.

21. Request shaping

One of the biggest hidden leaks is not just what the wallet asks for, but how it asks.

So request shaping should minimize:

uniquely small or targeted fetches,

sharp timing correlation with user action,

repeated address- or note-specific query forms,

and deterministic sync intervals.

The exact technique remains open, but the rule is fixed:

wallet requests must be shaped for ambiguity, not just efficiency.

22. Relay/provider separation

Where possible, the network design should separate:

the entity that relays transactions,

from the entity that serves state data,

from the entity that helps with note-candidate retrieval.

Reason:
one actor seeing every category of wallet behavior becomes too powerful.

The architecture should prefer compartmentalization of visibility.

23. Confirmation visibility

Wallets need to learn whether:

a transaction was accepted into provisional history,

it survived further work accumulation,

and it crossed user-facing confidence thresholds.

That status should be obtainable without forcing the wallet to reveal:

the full wallet,

or every related transaction.

So confirmation checking must be designed as part of the privacy model, not as a bolt-on convenience feature.

24. Logging and observability

Node operators and wallet developers will want logs and telemetry.

That is a danger.

The network layer must define privacy-safe observability principles:

no default logs that reconstruct user transaction intent,

no verbose request logs that expose wallet patterns,

minimized retention,

and clear separation between operational diagnostics and user surveillance.

25. Failure modes

This network architecture openly admits these failure risks.

25.1 Partial global observer advantage

A strong observer may still infer patterns despite protocol defenses.

25.2 Relay centralization

Privacy relays may centralize around a few providers.

25.3 Mobile privacy degradation

Mobile devices may fall back too often into weaker metadata privacy.

25.4 Request-shape leakage

Wallet fetch patterns may remain too revealing.

25.5 Eclipse despite diversity rules

A determined attacker may still isolate some clients.

25.6 Latency backlash

Users may disable privacy features if latency feels intolerable.
